    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="dc771ab2824940fc925222f97345f647"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="dc771ab2824940fc925222f97345f647">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'b15fb158-c7a0-40dd-9d0d-df14b6df91e3'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

                     <p>School of Chemistry, UNSW, 2052 Sydney, NSW, Australia</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Sharma/Neeraj">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span></div>
         </div>
      </div>
   </div>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d14778628" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>The electrochemical and thermal evolution of P2 Na<sub>2/3</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> is explored. The thermal phase transformations were found to be very similar irrespective of the state of charge with the formation of secondary phases, followed decomposition into NaF, Mn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> and MnO. These insights contribute to understanding the stability of P2 Na<sub>2/3</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e84bc4c1-a7b3-4c77-a655-18d11a16fcd3/cphc202400832-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/e84bc4c1-a7b3-4c77-a655-18d11a16fcd3/cphc202400832-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2f178831-f124-4321-8caa-a9f53e009f3c/cphc202400832-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e84bc4c1-a7b3-4c77-a655-18d11a16fcd3/cphc202400832-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d14778631" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>P2 Na<sub>2/3</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> can be used as a cathode material in sodium-ion batteries. Here, the electrochemical-temperature-dependent evolution of P2 Na<sub>2/3</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> is investigated using X-ray powder diffraction. P2 Na<sub>2/3</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> powder under a N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere shows evidence of the formation of a monoclinic <i>C</i>2/<i>m</i> phase, from about 450C. The P2 Na<sub>2/3</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> electrode sealed in a capillary undergoes a sequence of phase transitions from the as-prepared hexagonal <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub><i>/mmc</i> to a secondary hexagonal <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub><i>/mmc</i> phase followed by a transition to Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and subsequently MnO. NaF also appears parallel to the formation of the secondary hexagonal phase. These transitions suggest a local reducing environment as the Mn oxidation state evolves from 3<sup>+</sup>/4<sup>+</sup> to 2<sup>+</sup>. The samples at various states of charge show similar thermal evolution with the exception of the discharged (Na-inserted) state which features a slightly more complex evolution. Understanding the structure and thermal evolution at various states of charge and under various conditions provides insight into the stability of these potential cathode materials.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cphc202400832-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cphc202400832-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Lithium-ion batteries (LIB) are currently one of the most widely used rechargeable batteries due to their ability to store high amounts of energy.<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0001_R_d14778620e454" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem.l Soc.">1</a></sup></span> Their high energy density makes them attractive for various applications, from grid-scale energy storage systems to hybrid and electric vehicles to small-scale mobile electronic devices.<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0002_R_d14778620e457" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2">2</a></sup></span> However, growing concerns about safety, environmental impact, production cost, and scalability have prompted scientists to look for alternative candidates to overcome these limitations.<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0001_R_d14778620e460" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem.l Soc.">1</a></sup></span> Therefore, in recent years, significant attention has been directed towards developing new battery chemistries and materials that can offer better performance and safety while being cost-effective and environmentally sustainable.<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0003_R_d14778620e463" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - J Electrochem Soc">3</a>-<a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0005_R_d14778620e463" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy Environ. Sci.">5</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (NIBs) have emerged as a promising alternative due to the high abundance of sodium in the Earth's crust, which makes them potentially cost-effective.<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0006_R_d14778620e468" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Angewandte Chemie">6</a></sup></span> NIBs also show a relatively high redox potential, Na<sup>+</sup>/Na with E=2.71V relative to the standard hydrogen electrode, which is only slightly lower than that of lithium, Li<sup>+</sup>/Li with E=3.04V.<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0007_R_d14778620e475" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7">7</a></sup></span> However, the lower energy density of NIBs remains a major challenge for certain applications.<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0008_R_d14778620e478" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - J Phys. Chem. Lett.">8</a></sup></span> To address key challenges and enhance other essential parameters for improved performance, battery components need to be developed and optimised, for example, energy-dense electrodes that permit safe operation.<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0009_R_d14778620e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Batteries">9</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>In this regard, the development of efficient and safe cathode materials is critical for the performance and safety of NIBs.<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0003_R_d14778620e487" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - J Electrochem Soc">3</a>, <a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0005_R_d14778620e487" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy Environ. Sci.">5</a></sup></span> Various materials have been explored as potential cathode materials, including layered transition metal oxides, phosphates,<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0010_R_d14778620e490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Mater.">10</a></sup></span> NASICON's,<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0011_R_d14778620e493" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Inorg. Mater.">11</a></sup></span> sodium transition metal fluorophosphates,<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0012_R_d14778620e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Chem. Mater.">12</a></sup></span> and Prussian blue analogs.<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0013_R_d14778620e499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J Power Sources">13</a></sup></span> The choice of cathode material largely depends on the application's specific requirements, such as energy density, power density, and stability.<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0003_R_d14778620e503" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - J Electrochem Soc">3</a></sup></span> For example, layered transition metal oxides have been extensively studied as cathode materials due to their high energy density.<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0005_R_d14778620e506" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy Environ. Sci.">5</a>, <a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0014_R_d14778620e506" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Inorg. Chem.">14</a></sup></span> They also offer good theoretical capacity and demonstrate high conductivity and fast diffusion kinetics, but in some cases, they suffer from poor cycle stability, which can limit their practical application.<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0003_R_d14778620e509" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - J Electrochem Soc">3</a>, <a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0015_R_d14778620e509" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Physica B+C">15</a></sup></span> The continual development and fundamental understanding of processes occurring in these cathode materials will allow for better cathode design.<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0016_R_d14778620e512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Interdisciplinary Mater.">16</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Layered transition metal oxides, Na<sub>x</sub><i>TM</i>O<sub>2</sub>, where <i>TM</i> is one or more transition metals<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0014_R_d14778620e524" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Inorg. Chem.">14</a>, <a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0017_R_d14778620e524" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Chem. Mater.">17</a></sup></span> are classified based on the environment surrounding the sodium ions and the <i>TM</i> oxide layers, which features edge sharing octahedra and specific stacking sequences.<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0015_R_d14778620e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Physica B+C">15</a></sup></span> For these layered compounds, there are two main classifications, O-type where the Na<sup>+</sup> sits in an octahedrally coordinated environment and P-type where the Na<sup>+</sup> sits in a prismatic environment. A number is included in the classification which refers to the number of <i>TM</i> oxide layers in the unit cell and this leads to the major phases that are often reported such as P2, P3, O2, and O3.<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0017_R_d14778620e539" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Chem. Mater.">17</a>, <a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0015_R_d14778620e539" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Physica B+C">15</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>In particular, sodium manganese oxides are an attractive electrode material due to their high energy density, for example, -NaMnO<sub>2</sub> delivers 190mAhg<sup>1</sup> at a rate of C/20,<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0018_R_d14778620e549" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J Am. Chem. Soc.">18</a></sup></span> and the abundance of manganese in the Earth's crust.<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0019_R_d14778620e552" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Anim. Reprod. Sci.">19</a></sup></span> The phase formed typically depends on the Na/Mn ratio.<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0006_R_d14778620e555" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Angewandte Chemie">6</a></sup></span> The O3 structure has a sodium stoichiometry of approximately 1, while in the P2 structure, the sodium stoichiometry typically ranges from 0.5 to 0.75.<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0020_R_d14778620e559" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J Mater Chem. A">20</a></sup></span> Even though O3 NaMnO<sub>2</sub> can show a high initial capacity (185mAhg<sup>1</sup>),<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0021_R_d14778620e566" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J Electrochem. Soc.">21</a></sup></span> its reversible capacity abruptly decreases from the second cycle onwards predominantly because of the substantial structural changes as a result of the extraction of sodium.<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0022_R_d14778620e569" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Crystals (Basel)">22</a></sup></span> P2 Na<sub>2/3</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> can deliver 175mAg<sup>1</sup> during its first discharge and typically exhibits better cycling performance and rate-capability than the O3 phases.<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0023_R_d14778620e579" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J Alloys Compd.">23</a></sup></span> The observed capacity of these compounds varies depending on the current rate and precise composition. Focusing on the P2 phases, they can also undergo phase transitions during cycling, which can impact electrochemical performance, e.g., reduce the capacity retention. One significant factor contributing to these phase transitions is the Jahn-Teller distortion of the Mn<sup>3+</sup> ion. The redox reaction of Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> with Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction causes changes in the MnO<sub>6</sub> and hence the structure which can lead to adverse impacts on the material's performance.<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0006_R_d14778620e593" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Angewandte Chemie">6</a></sup></span> As a result, researchers have focused on substitutional modifications to P2 Na<sub>2/3</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, such as replacing Mn with one or more metals, to shift the initial Mn ion oxidation state closer to +4.<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0017_R_d14778620e600" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Chem. Mater.">17</a>, <a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0024_R_d14778620e600" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Chem. Commun.">24</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>It is important to note that the phase that is formed can also be tailored by the synthesis procedure. Table<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#cphc202400832-tbl-0001">1</a> summarizes some of the reported annealing temperatures and methods that have been used for the synthesis of different phases of Na<sub>2/3</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>. For instance, using Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and Mn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> with heating to 1050C followed by rapid quenching was shown to stabilize the orthorhombic P2 phase (can be considered as a distorted P2 phase) while annealing at 700C followed by slow cooling to 500C and subsequent quenching stabilizes the P2 phase, a hexagonal phase.<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0006_R_d14778620e621" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Angewandte Chemie">6</a></sup></span> Interestingly, it was also noted that the phase formation differed based on the cooling rate, even when the annealing temperature and precursors remained constant. For instance, annealing at 900C followed by slow cooling favored the formation of the P2 phase, whereas quenching promoted the formation of the P2 phase.<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0005_R_d14778620e624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy Environ. Sci.">5</a></sup></span> This example highlights the sensitivity of the resulting structure to the specific synthesis conditions. Moreover, the synthesis procedure's intricacies can lead to the emergence of a residual O3 (monoclinic) phase due to sodium loss during the heating process.<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0005_R_d14778620e627" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy Environ. Sci.">5</a></sup></span> This aspect underscores the multifaceted factors of the synthesis procedures that influence the final material composition and structure.
</p>

            <div class="article-section__table-source"></div>
         </div>
         
         <p>The solid-state method has been the most reported route to synthesize the P2 phase, with synthetic temperatures ranging from 600C and 1000C.<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0005_R_d14778620e1032" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy Environ. Sci.">5</a>, <a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0006_R_d14778620e1032" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Angewandte Chemie">6</a>, <a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0014_R_d14778620e1032" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Inorg. Chem.">14</a>, <a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0025_R_d14778620e1032" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J Power Sources">25</a>, <a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0026_R_d14778620e1032" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Nat Commun.">26</a>, <a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0024_R_d14778620e1032" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Chem. Commun.">24</a>, <a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0027_R_d14778620e1032" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Batteries">27</a></sup></span> Methods such as sol-gel (with a final heating step at 850C),<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0028_R_d14778620e1035" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - J. Mater. Chem. A">28</a>-<a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0030_R_d14778620e1035" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">30</a></sup></span> co-precipitation (with a final heating step at 720C),<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0031_R_d14778620e1038" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Nano Res.">31</a></sup></span> and ultrasonically assisted spray pyrolysis (with a final heating step at 800C)<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0032_R_d14778620e1041" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Mater. Chem. A">32</a></sup></span> have also been employed, see Table<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#cphc202400832-tbl-0001">1</a>. Determining an accurate phase composition after synthesis is crucial for a comprehensive understanding of the relationship between electrochemistry and crystallography.<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0026_R_d14778620e1048" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Nat Commun.">26</a></sup></span> While numerous studies utilizing solid-state synthesis at 900C have successfully produced the P2 phase with high purity,<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0026_R_d14778620e1051" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Nat Commun.">26</a>, <a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0024_R_d14778620e1051" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Chem. Commun.">24</a>, <a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0027_R_d14778620e1051" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Batteries">27</a></sup></span> some have reported the presence of a residual monoclinic phase at this temperature, accounting for approximately 20% of the phase fraction<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0005_R_d14778620e1054" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy Environ. Sci.">5</a></sup></span>. Furthermore, investigations into solid-state synthesis revealed a residual orthorhombic phase when annealed at both 1000C<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0014_R_d14778620e1057" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Inorg. Chem.">14</a></sup></span> and 600C.<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0025_R_d14778620e1060" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J Power Sources">25</a></sup></span> Even though P2 Na<sub>2/3</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> may appear to be a straightforward composition, the flexibility in Na content, loss of Na at high temperatures and variable Mn oxidation state are highly correlated which can lead to the formation of many related structures that in turn require careful characterization.<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0005_R_d14778620e1068" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy Environ. Sci.">5</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Several studies have been dedicated to developing models and gaining insights into the thermal behaviour of layered Na<sub>x</sub><i>TM</i>O<sub>2</sub> compounds after synthesis or after electrochemical cycling.<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0026_R_d14778620e1078" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Nat Commun.">26</a>, <a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0034_R_d14778620e1078" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Sci. Rep.">34</a>, <a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0035_R_d14778620e1078" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Chem. Mater.">35</a></sup></span> Thermal analysis was conducted on both P2 and O3 Na<sub>x</sub>MO<sub>2</sub> (M=Ti, Cr, Mn, Co, Ni) to examine the temperature dependence of lattice parameters to determine the thermal expansion coefficient and the evolution of the oxygen position parameters. Experiments were carried out from room temperature to 800K showing linear thermal expansion coefficients ranging from 0.481.310<sup>5</sup>K<sup>1</sup> and 1.372.7210<sup>5</sup>K<sup>1</sup> in the <i>a</i> and <i>c</i> axes respectively. No evidence of phase transitions was noted and the authors constructed a hard sphere model to develop a theoretical model for expansion properties.<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0034_R_d14778620e1099" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Sci. Rep.">34</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>A comprehensive study of the thermal properties of layered Na<sub>x</sub>VO<sub>2</sub> (ranging from room temperature to 270C) was conducted, alongside an investigation into its electrochemical phase evolution within the range of 0.51. This study led to the development of an extensive phase diagram. The findings suggest that the complexity of electrochemical and thermal phase evolution in Na<sub>x</sub>VO<sub>2</sub> arises from the ordered arrangement of sodium/vacancy between the metal oxide layers, driven by the relatively large ionic radius of Na ions and the possibility of the metal oxide layer gliding. This work has identified transformations within Na<sub>x</sub>VO<sub>2</sub>, which can be categorized as either irreversible or reversible. Briefly, the O3-NaVO<sub>2</sub> converts into O3-Na<sub>1/2</sub>VO<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>0.55</sub>VO<sub>2</sub>, and O3-Na<sub>2/3</sub>VO<sub>2</sub> via cell cycling and selected compositions were studied as a function of temperature. Low Na-content materials, O3-Na<sub>1/2</sub>VO2 and O3-Na<sub>0.55</sub>VO<sub>2</sub> undergo an O3P3 transition after heating to 200C. Further heating up to 270C results in a reversible transformation to the P3 structure. However, the Na-rich O3-Na<sub>2/3</sub>VO<sub>2</sub> does not exhibit any structural changes under the same treatment conditions.<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0035_R_d14778620e1143" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Chem. Mater.">35</a></sup></span> Notably, this study yielded novel metastable phases of Na<sub>x</sub>VO<sub>2</sub> through sodium electrochemical (de)intercalation at room temperature, phases that were previously inaccessible via conventional synthesis methods. Many works investigate the thermal properties of Na<sub>x</sub><i>TM</i>O<sub>2</sub> using techniques such as thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) or differential scanning calorimetry (DSC). For example, combining XRD with TGA, P2 Na<sub>0.67</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> was shown to readily exchange Na<sup>+</sup> with H<sup>+</sup> by absorbing moisture from the atmosphere forming hydrated Na<sub>0.67</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>. The TGA experiment on the hydrated P2 Na<sub>0.67</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> showed dehydration and decomposition of NaHCO<sub>3</sub> within the temperature range of 70130C, deprotonation of Na<sub>0.67-x</sub>H<sub>x</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> occurring between 130217C, decomposition of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> at temperatures above 130C, and the recrystallization process of the P2 phase at temperatures exceeding 483C. Work on hydrated P2 Na<sub>0.67</sub>Zn<sub>0.1</sub>Mn<sub>0.9</sub>O<sub>2</sub> showed differences to hydrated P2 Na<sub>0.67</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, e.g., the recrystallization temperature of the P2-type Na<sub>0.67</sub>Zn<sub>0.1</sub>Mn<sub>0.9</sub>O<sub>2</sub> phase was found to be lower at 376C and this was coupled with related work on Na<sub>0.67</sub><i>TM</i><sub>0.1</sub>Mn<sub>0.9</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. This work was trying to show that hydrated Na<sub>0.67</sub>MO<sub>2</sub> samples can be restored to their original structures through high-temperature annealing.<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0026_R_d14778620e1221" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Nat Commun.">26</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Here, following synthesis, and structural and electrochemical characterization of P2 Na<sub>2/3</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, its thermal evolution is determined. Furthermore, the thermal evolution of P2 Na<sub>2/3</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> at various states of charge and discharge, i.e., varying Na content, is determined and the evolution is compared across the series. The phase transitions and their associated weight fractions, and lattice parameter evolution for state of charge and temperature allows us to develop a comprehensive understanding of the electrochemical-thermal phase evolution.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cphc202400832-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cphc202400832-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The synthesis procedure of P2 Na<sub>2/3</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> was optimised after experimenting with several different annealing procedures, and the XRD data related to this process are shown in FigureS1. The resulting SEM and EDX data for P2 Na<sub>2/3</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> are shown in FigureS2 and TableS1. The Rietveld-refined fit of the P2 Na<sub>2/3</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> structural model to the XRD data is shown in FigureS3 and the refined structural data is presented in TableS2. A minor impurity may be present as evident by the weak shoulder on the 004 and 100 reflections. P2 Na<sub>2/3</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> adopts <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub><i>/mmc</i> space group symmetry with refined lattice parameters of <i>a</i>=2.864(3) and <i>c</i>=11.1390(3) which are similar to those reported by Liu etal.<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0029_R_d14778620e1271" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Angewandte Chemie">29</a></sup></span> The lattice parameters of some of the previously reported Na<sub>x</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> phases (where 0.5&lt;x&lt;0.8) have been summarized in TableS3 for comparison. The electrochemical performance of a P2 Na<sub>2/3</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> based half-cell is shown in FigureS4. The initial discharge capacity was 168mAhg<sup>1</sup>, which decreased to 123mAhg<sup>1</sup> at the 23<sup>rd</sup> cycle and then slowly decreased to 72mAhg<sup>1</sup> by the 100<sup>th</sup> cycle.</p>
         
         <p>The thermal evolution of the as-prepared powder of P2 Na<sub>2/3</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> under a N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere is shown in Figure<a href="#cphc202400832-fig-0001">1a</a>. Rietveld analysis revealed that the P2 Na<sub>2/3</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> phase is present throughout the temperature range up to 750C. The lattice parameters and volume expand with temperature. However, the rate of expansion experiences a notable change around 450C, which correlates to the emergence of a new phase. Various structural models were used to describe the secondary phase with the best fit being a monoclinic Na<sub>x</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> model with <i>C</i>2/<i>m</i> space group symmetry.<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0036_R_d14778620e1318" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - J Solid State Chem">36</a></sup></span> Figure<a href="#cphc202400832-fig-0001">1b</a> shows the Rietveld refined fit of the structural models with XRD data at 450C where both phases distinctively co-exist. Figure<a href="#cphc202400832-fig-0001">1c</a> shows the evolution of 002 and 001 reflections for the hexagonal and monoclinic phases respectively, during the heat treatment. The evolution of the lattice parameters, volume, and weight fractions of both phases are shown in Figure<a href="#cphc202400832-fig-0002">2</a> and S5a. The monoclinic <i>C</i>2/<i>m</i> phase gradually increases at the expense of the hexagonal <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub><i>/mmc</i> phase reaching over 50% by 750C. The transition from a hexagonal <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub><i>/mmc</i> or P2 to a monoclinic <i>C</i>2/<i>m</i> phase with temperature is unusual as most compounds transform from low symmetry to higher symmetry structures with temperature.<span><sup><a href="#cphc202400832-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cphc202400832-bib-0025_R_d14778620e1351" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J Power Sources">25</a></sup></span> However, as shown in Table<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#cphc202400832-tbl-0001">1</a> and S3 there appear to be a variety of possible Na<sub>x</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> phases that can form around this composition and within an open N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere, Na-loss and/or slight Mn reduction during thermal treatment cannot be fully discounted. Considering that the monoclinic <i>C</i>2/<i>m</i> phase is often reported as being Na-rich, the mechanism may involve the formation of monoclinic Na<sub>~1</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> with a correlated loss of Na in the hexagonal phase, i.e., Na<sub>2/3-x</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>. This correlates with a distinct expansion in the <i>c</i> lattice parameter for the hexagonal phase between 400500C (Figure<a href="#cphc202400832-fig-0002">2</a>), noting that a reduction in the Na concentration results in an expansion of the <i>c</i> lattice parameter due to the increased repulsion from neighbouring O in the <i>TM</i>O<sub>6</sub> layers. It should be noted that at the highest temperatures of 750C, a small proportion of Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> can be modelled. Upon cooling to room temperature a combination of hexagonal, monoclinic Na<sub>x</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> and a minute quantity of Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> is observed, see FigureS5b.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="cphc202400832-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c77ad5e6-a518-40ed-ace7-3edfa59c7907/cphc202400832-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/c77ad5e6-a518-40ed-ace7-3edfa59c7907/cphc202400832-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2e300805-8e07-4dea-b8bc-b1b8faf07573/cphc202400832-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c77ad5e6-a518-40ed-ace7-3edfa59c7907/cphc202400832-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
